RATING: 6
(12/07): I don't think there are many games out there with the constant amount of negotiation and trickery present in Traders of Genoa. The movement mechanic is inspired and the random shortening of the game (without the game ending suddenly) puts a unique pressure on the players that I haven't seen in any other game I've played so far.

I certainly enjoy myself when I play Traders of Genoa, but having a player basically decide the game via an unwittingly lopsided trade leaves a sour taste in my mouth at times. I'd probably enjoy this more with a different group of people, but for now, I only care to play occasionally.

RATING: 6
1st Play: I don't remember it very well, it's been quite a while, but I know that I generally enjoyed the game with fast negotiations and tough to complete goals.

2nd Play: It's been long enough since my first play that I had to relearn the rules. The game is an excellent negotiation game, and I like all of the back and forth trading and banter that happens during the game. It's tough to place a value on some of the spaces and I like that, the tension drives the game. Unfortunately it goes on a little long - with a good group, it runs 2 hours, and the final turn felt a bit anti-climatic compared to the action that happened in the previous rounds.

RATING: 7
3-5(5)

I think this one's a bit long for what it is, and it takes a couple of plays to really understand the negotiation aspects of the game. Namely, that pretty much anything is negotiable. The winning is a bit abstract, in that you can gain as much from selling off your position as the person buying it from you might. And then there's the standard pratfall of negotiation games with people refusing to deal under any circumstance.

Still, I think it's a pretty solid entry for a trading / negotiation game.

RATING: 7
{1 play, 5 player} First play, which is usually tough in a auction-ish game.  I felt like I might be out of it early, as it was slow going, trying to build up resources.  But about a third of the way through, the lightbulb went off (somewhat) and I could see now that this was more of a long haul and not just quick hits.  Then, about the time I started being able to value things, we switched over to a timer to move things along (fine idea) and by the end of the game, I found myself ready to play again.  Unlike other trading games, I actually felt like I was trading for *something* (as opposed to a bigger share of profits, or a coin for a coin, etc), I was trading for strategy, as well as VP's.  I agree with others, however, in that player's agendas are mostly hidden... and really, everything's good, so there may not be much skill to trading, even though it feels like there is.  But I can certainly see where haggling over $5 could drive some crazy - especially since, without a timer, this baby can drag.  Hell, even with a timer.  I think Chinatown and I'm the Boss are more fun (and certainly quicker), but this one's more meaty - or, at least, it felt that way.

RATING: 8
Rating based on just one play.

I think this rating will go up with repeated plays.  The game was always fun, but it started out cautiously as we learned how things worked.  Near the end I started to get a better sense of how to value different goods and alternate ways of approaching the game.

I like other negotiation games like Bohnanza and I'm The Boss!.  Traders of Genoa offers that same trading and negotiation but in a much more complex and deep package.

For one, the values of the goods you trade are not always obvious, so you must figure that out like you do in many auction/eurogames.  There are also many different kinds of resources that score in different ways, as well as resources that convert other resources into different types.  

Second, I liked the mechanic offered to determine what resources would be available in a given round.  This required some thinking ahead to plan/manipulate the path so that it hit the buildings you needed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Excellent Negotiation game. May be a little too long for a game of this sort but you always feel involved and there are MANY paths to victory.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There are so many context and player-specific hidden valuations that negotiating becomes more than just open math.  The rules aren't that hairy overall, but the playtime has an understandable tendency to keep this one off the table. Especially if you're not rolling 4/5 combos to expedite the game!  I don't consider it to be an epic, but I think I've come around to the idea that this game is three hours well spent if it ends up taking that long.  Very curious about playing it with 2.  Best of breed for negotiation games in my book so far. Game system has a tendency to promote overbids among gamers because of EV thoughts: "I can bid 20 to make 30" - well, you actually gave the merchant 20 on everyone else while only gaining 10 yourself!

RATING: N/A
The Traders of Genoa
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
After first play: Rules have to be the near epitome of convoluted English. It must be easier to be concise rather than  repeating the same point twice with equally cumbersome language. As a result we struggled.

Eventually launched into the game and got to grips with it. The game isn't that hard, why did the rules try to make it so? Fundamentally this game relies heavily on player interaction. You must broker deals to complete your orders and deliver letters. It is not sufficient to wait until your go to hope to do your deals then. 

My group are cut throat, there is no cooperation if there is the possibility that the other player will benifit a penny more out of a deal. As a result I think the game thrives on a more friendly cooperative set of players as we choked the life out of it by refusing low offers for actions.

One play is not enough to judge this in a balanced way. Clearly there is a clever mechanism here but at this stage I'm surprised the game is ranked so highly.

RATING: 9
If you don't hate trading games, you must give this a try.

It can be a little bit long (considering you do the same things over and over) but it's quite the game. Very balanced and interesting mechanics. About as heavy as a trading game could possibly be (any heavier would be bad because of the inherent balance issues a trading game can have).

I do find it annoying that the base number for the game is 5 (why not 1, 2, 8, 20 bills? much easier...) and the paper money that comes with the Alea edition is really really crappy. The one from the new Filosofia edition is much nicer, even if the box is mightily oversized)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Not bad for a negotiation game.  I need to stop accepting offers for moves/actions after I reach my desired spot; it's just not worth selling actions to your opponents for 5 or 10 bucks each when ending your turn early (thus denying them the actions) will slow opponents considerably.  I'm a sucker for the privilege cards... they are one of the most random parts of the game, but I'm realizing that negotiations should center around them since there are tons of points to be had with sets of privilege cards.  The trick with them will be determining a fair value for trades.  There's also a lot to be said for the shop ownership tokens (from the Cathedral); they are very powerful when used on the central buildings... and when combined with the "building action" card from the Palace.  Almost everything is negotiable, which makes the game pretty free-form... naturally it turns into a "hurt the leader" game... the downside of free-form is having to play favorites when you get equal offers.

RATING: 4
I enjoy Chinatown - a game that Genoa is often compared to - quite a bit, so it's not as if I just have have a standing dislike for negotiation games. I think that difference is that the "goods" in Chinatown are much more limited so it's much easier to calculate the value of trades. In Genoa, one can trade goods, cards, buildings, and special tiles, and money, leading to the potential that trades will be a mishmash of this, that, and the other thing with no clear idea of where the value lies. One might as well toss the bits i nthe air and grab a pile at random.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Not our kind of game, though a cool concept: a resource-management, strategy Euro whose central mechanic is negotiation. Sadly, I don't think the blend works very well here. 

Negotiation games are predicated on imbalances and group dynamics (i.e. people conspire to stop a strong leader), but in Genoa, players tend to simply go for what is best for them without working hard enough to stop the player in the lead. This is because there is so much hidden information in this game (Orders, Messages, Privileges), that it's often difficult to tell who is leading until it's too late. Additionally, the fact that the game can end very suddenly based on a couple of die rolls also means that long-term planning (and general knowledge of the game state) is impossible. So, it's an interesting idea, but ultimately a flawed one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Well, I can certainly say that I'm a fan of negotiation games. Rated this an 8 after one play with five people. I can pretty much guarantee that not everyone shares my same view; with negotiation, some people love it, and some don't.

Any negotiation game needs something that drives the bargaining. In Genoa, it's "the tower." You need something? You need me to move that tower to your spot. It's a great mechanism for driving people to hagggle.

A few drawbacks. Our game clocked in at three hours. Yeesh. For me, the time flew by. But that's a big commitment. I gotta think that the game will flow smoother with familiarity, but any negotiation can potentially drag depending on the personalities at the table. 

If you're a fan of negotiation games, you should give this a shot. There's not many "big box" games like it.

Update: 2nd play, and I still like it, and it still takes too long. Another five player game, this time with great fellow gamers that are not slow players, and it still took about three hours. Next time, I'd be very tempted to simply shave off a round at the start. I can't think of any significant drawbacks, and I don't think the "game arc" or narrative would suffer for it, even if we rolled the market and lost a turn on top of that. It's a very intriguing negotiation game, but one that's too taxing at its current length.

Update: after a third play, I decided to trade this one away. Again, I was struck by the slow pacing of this game. I love the "tower" as a mechanic for putting the current player in the driver's seat for negotiations, but this game strikes me as lasting a bit too long, with a bit too much clutter with all the various commodities, contracts, etc. Although this is supposed to be the negotiation game for gamers, I find myself rather wanting to get Chinatown to the table instead. There's a lot to like with this game, but the few things that irk me means I don't need to own it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm not the biggest fan of pure negotiation games, and I'd rather not play them too often--when I'm not really in the hardball negotiation mindset, I tend to almost give away trades to anyone who asks--but I enjoy this one quite a bit.  There's money exchanged every step of the way, and one must definitely make the most of vested interests.  Several different moneymaking strategies are viable, so it's possible to see a close game between players who're using completely different strategies--fun and refreshing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We play this 2-player.  Negotiation games can fall flat with 2, but this one offers a nice incentive for trading.  If you accept a trade offer from your opponent then you get to take a bonus action.  There's a lengthy rulebook to plow through before you can get started.  Our first game, including reading the rules, took nearly 3 hours.  Our second game (with a review of the rules) shaved an hour off that.  Now it only takes about an hour.  Once you understand the rules, they really aren't all that complicated.  This game mixes negotiations with pick-up and deliver and set collecting.  There are lots of ways to attack it.  You can choose what to focus your efforts on based on what you prefer and how the game plays out.  With 2 players, taking Privilege cards is very important.  This is quite an engaging game; everyone can be involved, regardless of whose turn it is.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Update: It just jumped to a 9 on the second play. Now, if we can get it in in 2 1/2 hours, it would be a solid 10. Best commercial negotiation game that I know of by a long shot. (Diplomacy is still the #1 strategic negotiation game in my book, even though I rate it lower. Just too long.)

Months after playing this for the first time, I can't stop thinking about it. I love how the different ways of scoring creates a spread between player valuations for the same items. It is this spread that makes the negotiations fresh. I don't like to rate things higher than an 8 before a second play, but if I did, this would likely be a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Everything can be and is frequently traded.  Shanghai-style free-form bazaar. No downtime.  Maintain a larger average vig.  Actual values of items are mostly soft and mostly relative.  Exploit trade deltas.

While the rules declare that the tower route should be sold iteratively from the start space, the game is considerably improved if the entire route is sold and agreed in advance of actually putting the tower down and doing anything.  This can extend as far as also selling the location chosen for the StartAnywhere card.  Thus a turn could easily start with a statement like: "I'm using a StartAnywhere and at some point I'm going to the Harbour -- who wants to go where?" and then leaving everything about the entire turn, including whether or not you even go to the harbour open to sale and negotiation.

Ohh, and trade goods and cards more readily than money.  Really.  They're both represent only potential value and have a higher gradient value than cash.  Exploit that fact.

Also improved by playing with open money via poker chips.

RATING: 9
:tobacco: :( :what: :what: :D :D UPDATE: After a second run-through (one that actually finished up in a reasonable time no less) I feel more confidence recommending this game, but really only for those who share my predilection for negotiation/haggling games. I feel we left a lot of the game's strategic space unexplored (almost all our trading was cash-for-actions, which led to the game featuring a lot of moves that ended up with a net profit of zero at best) but I think there's a lot of game here. If I had to guess (and I'm DEFINITELY no expert), the key to the game is creative uses for the special tiles, creating positive-sum swaps (win-wins where you win more, ideally) and setting yourself up as the merchant to get people to pay you to take actions you'd do anyway.

---


I've been on a bit of an anti-Euro kick of late. More accurately I've been on an anti-Euro-as-the-term-is-defined-now-in-2015 kick; I'll bounce back but at the moment I'm just burned out on blocking-as-interaction/action-efficiency/multiplayer solitaire/worker-placement/trade-a-pink-cube-for-a-white-cube/the whole megillah.

So forgive me for being a bit leery when a friend brought Traders of Genoa (we played the Alea edition, what of it) to game night. I mean, German designer? Yep. Renaissance Apple Merchant theme, roughly applied with borrowed Glu-Stik? Ooh yes. Blandly named for one port city or another? Hooah. Picture of a dour ofay wearing a silly hat on the box cover? Just so. My eyes glaze over at the thought.

And yet -- very much not that in the end. I mean, yes, sure pasted on theme, but the theme (along with everything else about the game) is just a minimal framework which gives an ultimate purpose and direction to the real game: [b]negotiating, bargaining and bidding over every goddamned thing under the sun[/b]. Everything you collect in the game -- money, goods, special powers -- is just an asset. Some assets you care about more than others. Some assets others care about more than you.

Except money. Everybody needs money. That's why they call it "money."

And everything that happens in the game -- where you go and whether you can actually do anything when you get there, to pick two examples -- is subject to debate, bickering, and, ultimately, somebody standing up and saying Eff You Pay Me Or Nobody Gets Nothing.

And -- oh yes -- the game is ruthlessly designed to give players leverage and change the equation of that leverage with every small movement.

So, leverage and arbitrage. Arbitrage and leverage. A giant swap meet where pretty much everything goes.

Is this everyone's idea of fun? I am already compiling a list of people I am morally certain will hate the whole experience. But I, after playing a game that was aborted somewhere near the end of the first trimester, am intrigued. My hesitation is that the experience might get a little long for what it is. (The box claims a playtime of 60 minutes is possible; to call this a polite understatement is itself a bit of a polite understatement.) I would budget 3 hours and if you're getting it done more quickly, you're probably leaving money on the table as a group. And play this with people who like to flap their gums and strike deals, or you're in for a pretty grim time; this isn't a game like Tammany Hall that's fun whether your group likes to negotiate or not.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
If you love free-form negotiation games, this one is for you.  We play that anything and everything can be traded.  WE even allow the promise of a future action, though we allow those deals to be "forgotten" to create an environment of spite and retribution that really forces somebody to weigh the cost benefit ratio of their action.  The game is about making the right deal to increase the progress towards your objective (at the time) while making deals sweet enough to entice others to give you that bigger slice of the pie.  Trouble is, you don't always know if you are helping your trading partner more than yourself.

In the end, I hoped this would be my go-to negotiation game, but I never had the right group for it.  Lifeboats and Bohnanza filled the niche and was more their speed.  Traded

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like this game, it's balanced, has lots of options, and looks great. I recall being somewhat kerfuddled about how much I liked this game when I first sat down to it, but it eventually grew on me. Exceptional in that there are an abundant number of different ways to approach it successfully. Small Orders are easier to cash in than large orders, but offer no special chits. Cathedral tokens to "own" buildings offer some needed flexibility and income, but they also tend to make you a target. The Post Office message cards don't pay off a lot, but they are very easy to meet. And so on. Another interesting thing that I like about this game is that it is rather accessible for a heavy weight game. I have used this as an introductory game with much success, and I believe a goodly part of the reason why it works is that even though there are a lot of ways to win, the end goal, making money, is always intelligible. In addition to that, the game is very, very social, and it keeps players involved constantly because of the mechanism whereby you are negotiating for the right to take actions on other players turns. When you add this to the hidden victory points, you get a complicated game with plenty of room for nuanced play which nonetheless keeps new gamers involved and never makes them feel as though they're out of the race. However, the game depends heavily on the group to sink or swim. With people who are moderately social the game is only decent, while with a raucous group of 4 or 5 who love to haggle it's unbelievably good. If you have more of the latter than the former this game is a must buy, for others it's recommended to try it first. One major complaint: Not nearly enough money is included in the box, five player games often end with a cleaned out bank, is it too much to ask for two pads of cash instead of the meager one?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Fairly typical light euro. Main mechanic is that the active player sells the opportunity to do (up to five) actions which are geographically constrained. The path to victory is (largely) collecting sets of goods and trading them in for points, though as long as a player does some of this other point gaining activities can be fruitfully used. The randomness is small (in a broad sense), but as annoying as the randomness in Puerto Rico.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I bought this game when I was still in the habit of buying games unplayed to expand my collection, trusting well-known sites (not this one, hadn't discovered it yet) to provide me with objective information. And with this game on everyone's 'must have' list, I figured I couldn't go wrong. Welllll... after a game or two, I was't too sure. For one thing, the game's sole focus on trade bored me. Everything is for sale, and the trick is to value stuff better than your competitors do. I'm sure there's an art to it, but it's not one I found very interesting. Second, once the rush of learning the title ebbed away (which is fun to do because there are tons of little things to try) I found the actual game of trading, trading and trading some more annoying, especially in the groups I played it with. My fellow players are quick bargainers, but they are also always on the lookout to squeeze blood from a stone. Traders is a game of give and take, not a game of take and take, and this sapped the fun out of the proceedings quicker than a sand desert soaks up water. It helped that people can force things along by not moving the disc tower, but if this happens too often the mood quickly turns sour. Finally, no matter what group I played with, the last few turns were very boring as noone was going to spend money on assignments they knew they were not likely to complete anyway. Everyone just wants the game to end; personally I think the game lasts for 2 or even 3 rounds too many. The game is a 6 for the first 2/3rds of the game; and a meagre 4 for the last 1/3rd—average is about 5. I wanted to like this when it was still early in my discovery of modern boardgames, but it turned out the be one of the earliest failures. A shame, but shit happens. The game never got played after the initial attempts, and was let go late 2009.

[b]Update[/b] A few replays over the years with someone else's copy cemented the cold harsh reality that negotiation games are not meant for me nor the groups I play with. I dislike their artificialness and cannot make myself become someone who will haggle for stuff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Production: Lots of well-made bits, and an easy-to-understand board.  The art design on the cards was uncreative, and I would have appreciated some symbology on the special action tiles to acclimate me to their powers.  Play: There’s a lot going on in this game, as each player is usually working toward a unique objective at any given time, but their needs cross paths, making for interesting bargaining.  I think the game could be simplified a bit (4 commodities instead of 8, for instance) without losing its potential for interaction and conflict; I suppose I just felt a bit lost on my first playing because there were so many options from which to choose. RATING: 9

If you like Settlers of Catan but find yourself wanting to do more in terms of trades, you should pick this one up.  My only problem with it is the trading can really drag as players hold out for better deals.  Maybe a sandtimer is needed in order to speed it up.

RATING: 6
Hard to rate this one. Hard to rate negotiation games in general, as it is more about the players than the game, and the game just provides a context for the players to trade around. 

The game is long, and (unlike Mall of Horror) I can't say that all the mechanisms add new dimensions to the trading. But it doesn't detract from it either, and doesn't "get in the way" or feel like chrome for the sake of chrome. Whatever complexity there is, while not devilishly clever, doesn't feel unnecessary either and works. For that, I'll give it an 8. But honestly, I wouldn't want to play this, or any negotiation game, very often.

RATING: 7
Loved my first game of Traders of Genoa. Bought a copy after playing Traders of Genoa.

I played twice so far with my copy of Traders of Genoa. I feel the same way as my first game. I enjoy Traders of Genoa quite a lot, though possibly it's not a game for everyone since there is a lot of negotiations during the game. Some can be turned off by that. Everyone has their taste in board games. The players you play with at Traders of Genoa adds to the enjoyability of the game. Either hit or miss.

The gamers I played with had a good time playing this game. Lots of negotiations and jokes took place when dealing with the tower player. Influencing the tower player with the different offers and trying to convince him/her that your offer is the best is quite something to see at times. Good times!

Game plays a bit differently between a 4 player game and a 5 player game. One more player is more intense when dealing with the tower player. Not everyone can make actions every turn with 5 players. It makes negotiations more important if you want to make a profit and trying to win the game.

The more you deal in Traders of Genoa the better. I would want to play the game again to test some strategies in the game.

I would need to try the game with different people to see how negotiation changes from person to person.

Traded Traders of Genoa in a game trade. Something I might get back in my collection.

RATING: 7
Hmm, I thought I would like it more.  Trading is such a pure mechanic, and one that promises replayability and unpredictability.  I think part of my problem with the mechanic is that I want to play trading games more slowly - whereas I'm willing to make a computational error in an analytic game, I'm much more afraid of making a bad trade in a game like this.

I think this is a case where the theme could have been given more thought.  Wandering around an Italian city is not really an ideal setting for a cruel, wild game of anything goes - and the same old trade goods adds a fustiness to the proceedings.  Definitely seems like a game that I would enjoy more with more plays, a certain comfortability with the mechanics would probably allow for more complex negotiations.  I don't care much for the privilege option - we had one player go for broke on it and it sort of removed him from what everyone else was trying to do and had him rely on luck.  Normally multiple strategies are a good attribute, but I think in a game with open trading, it helps to have all the players trying to get roughly the same things to maximize bidding intrigue.

RATING: 4
This one is not my cup of team, but if you like negotiation games, then Genoa is a must for you to try. I was taken with the clever decisions that the game gives you and the number of options on your turn. Each turn you get to move the “cart”, which basically means you get to choose the spaces that will be in play this turn. A space may be simply a street space (which has its own implications) or a building which offers actions (the heart of the game). So you might choose to move to the buildings for actions, but here’s the catch – you can only take one of the actions. You auction off the remaining actions to your opponents. They can offer to share the benefit of the building or offer you something else entirely. 

Everything is liquid in this game and the combination of moves and trades you can make is staggering (oh, do you have options). It’s the strength and weakness of the game, IMO, because you want to explore all these options -- as each of these options involves negotiation -- but after a couple hours of min/maxing options and weighing offers, it gets tiresome and weary for me. You don’t want to rush players as the raison d'etre of the game is to consider the 101 things you can do each turn. 

So I liked the profundity it offered but at the same time, I wanted less. The two hour mark seems perfect for such a deep game so we could all really take time during our turns without the game getting to the point where it dragged, but this is a 3-4 hour beast.

RATING: 4
Unusual trading/negotiation and routing game. I liked how you sell off your extra actions and how you are fulfilling missions. I'm souring on this game because it seems to take forever...

Duration: There's little book-keeping and little downtime as you can bid on everyone else's turn, furthermore there is a turn limit. However wrangling can go on, so it's best to keep things moving.
Design: Unusual movement mechanic and action-selling mechanic.
Strategy: The privileges are pretty random, but the open negotiation lets you do a lot. You can collude and gang up to some extent, and there is the potential for metagaming.
Components: Workmanlike, not artistic in any way.
Theme: The mechanics fit the theme, especially messaging and sending rice to the restaurant. The theme is still another Renaissance European economic situation though.

Lot's of talking in this game, you don't sit quietly and build an economy like you do in many Euros.  This is a game about interacting with the other folks at the table and trying to get that tower to go your way.  Love the look and feel of the production.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Negotiation games aren't my cup of tea, and Genoa is about as negotiable as a game can possibly be. Everything is up for trade: Money, goods, contracts, privileges, actions, and even the steps to take on the board. Nothing is safe, nothing is sacred.

Since the game consists of nothing but bartering the key to victory is having a more precise appreciation of value than your opponents. That's all there is to it, and it can get a little repetitive after a while.

That said, unless you hate negotiation Genoa certainly deserves a try. It's clearly at the top of its class, and rightfully so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Something about this game shouldn't work, but it does! The negotiation aspect is just so open that it would be easy for the game to easily fall into a free-for-all shout fest of deals.  And sometimes that happens, but for me, it's an aspect of the game I enjoy. There is some luck in the game considering the multiple card draws, but overall there is a lot to think about and this game is perfect for those who enjoy negotiation, manipulation, trade, psych-outs, and all that good stuff that comes in a game like this. Not my favourite Dorn game (Goa is just so good!) but this one is a great one with the right crowd. I can definitely see people not enjoying it, however - even those who might like some other negotiation games. This game can leave you feeling raw!  Genoa is historically significant because it introduced Rudiger Dorn to the larger gaming world and also was the first instance of his 'tower' mechanic (seen later in games like Goa and Istanbul). That part is enjoyable, and so is the negotiation aspect. But I think the length of the game vs. what players get out of it is a bit unforgivable these days. The game, unfortunately, has no development. You play it for 2 hours and the first round feels exactly like the last round. Because nothing grows or develops on the board, you are simply taking each turn to complete the cards you currently have in your hand. Once complete, get new cards. Then repeat. This would feel good for a 1-hour game, but 2 hours is really stretching it. You're basically doing the same things over and over again. And the game can't be that much shorter because luck would play more of a part to it. The length is what balances the luck out for everyone and gives all players enough time to take enough efficient turns needed to make advances. I think this is an important game historically and I would play it again if others asked, but it's feeling a little dated now and Dorn quickly hit it out of the park in a much better way with Goa. I still can't think of a negotiation game that does negotiation as interestingly as Genoa does, so it will be hard to replace that aspect. But I would feel a little long in the tooth introducing this game to new players when there are much meatier and more interesting games to play if I had 2 hours to spare.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The purest of the negotiation games that I have played. This game is all player interaction, and can lend itself to some heated negotiations and debates on what are good deals.  Traders of Genoa can slow down and run long, but as opposed to some people I have no problem with this, as it usually means players are involved.  For me the biggest drawback is, in my experience, that inevitably someone feels left out - as if they were never considered in offers, and therefore never felt allowed to compete.  I am not sure if this is an absolute with the game, as it makes sense to me to try and diversify the deals so as not to let anyone player  to get too far ahead.  Aside from these, what I deem fixable irritants, I believe Traders to be an enjoyable game.  Highly interactive and tense. Best played with full compliment of players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The best trading game I have ever played.  Wow.  Loads of fun.  Enough going on alongside the trading to make it really interesting.  It's got movement, a constant stream of interesting decisions, and full-time engagement by all the players.

I'm going to quote Josh Miller to explain why it's so good:  "What elevates Traders of Genoa over many other pure negotiation games is that there's actually a lot of "game" surrounding the central activity of negotiation. Games like Chinatown, I'm The Boss, Intrige and Rette Sich Wer Kann (Lifeboats) are less interesting because the bare-bones structure makes the purse very easy to calculate. Traders of Genoa does a better job of clouding the water by using a more complex game system, more hidden information, and varying paths and goals among the players. Consequently, there's more to the game than persuading, coaxing, bullying, deflecting, begging, and threatening. There are also interesting game decisions to be made.'

RATING: 8
:star:
The best negotiation game I had played and probably the best one in the market. If I were a negotiation fan I would rate it with a 10 probably, but I am not. Game is easy to explain but difficult to play correctly. You will need many plays to do it well. Its an interactive fest, in which you can offer/trade everything, from money to goods and rewards. A superior game than Chinatown, but also more difficult to grasp and to play. Rules are pretty straightforward and clear, but in my opinion they need some corrections like the bonus for the fast turns as some people are just bad at negotiating and it takes them forever. In this case, it may not be a bad idea to set a timer or the game could drag with a/p players. As other games like Republic of Rome, Greed Inc or Princes of Renaissance, it needs a good talking and interactive group or could be a little bit boring. Played with the correct group a pretty entertaining experience, and if you love negotiation games, just the best pick you can make.

RATING: 9
Picked this up at Hairy Trantula in Toronto when up there for my Aniversary.  Tried it out last weekend, what a different interesting game.  This game uses a very interesting mechanic that reminds me just a bit of Puerto Rico.  It's like Puerto rico on each players turn every player may take one action.  Other then that though this game is very unique.  Players need to deliver goods and messages around the city of Genoa, the interesting part is that the Trader starts in a random place each turn and can only move 4 additional spaces, it's up to the player who's turn it is to decide where he should go and the other players Bribe the acting player trying to convince them to move the Trader to places that would be advantageous to them.  It's a very different style, and seems very open for a variety of different play styles.

RATING: 9
Traders of Genoa was a pleasant surprise for me.  It's almost completely a game of negotiation, and I'm not always crazy about that sort of game.  Early reports made the game sound like an open-ended marathon of attrition that would turn all but the diehard deal-makers into a shivering pool of goo.

But I tried it anyway, and I'm glad I did.  Yes, everything in the game is tradeable, but that breadth of possibilities has never caused the negotiations to drag on too long in our group.  Most deals can be arranged simply and quicly if the players don't get bogged down in minutiae.  However, there's definitely a particular breed of perfectionist with whom I'd avoid playing this game.

What elevates Traders of Genoa over many other pure negotiation games is that there's actually a lot of "game" surrounding the central activity of negotiation.  Games like Chinatown, I'm The Boss, Intrige and Rette Sich Wer Kann (Lifeboats) are less interesting because the bare-bones structure makes the purse very easy to calculate.  Traders of Genoa does a better job of clouding the water by using a more complex game system, more hidden information, and varying paths and goals among the players.  Consequently, there's more to the game than persuading, coaxing, bullying, deflecting, begging, and threatening.  There are also interesting game decisions to be made.

Traders of Genoa demands a combination of calculation, forethought, creativity, wit, guile, and a silver tongue.  That makes for a broad and involving game experience.  One of the main attractions of a good negotiation game is the way it allows players' personalities to shine through.  Traders of Genoa is especially good here because it leaves room for a wide array of personalities.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is somewhat bewildering at first, since there's so much going on, so many ways to score, and so much trade and bargaining. The combination of many paths to victory and extensive interaction between players in open trade about virtually everything, makes this one of the greatest games.

Perhaps if the game had been designed today, it would have been made more simple. The complexity also contributes to a long playing time. The ownership markers, for instance, while intriguing and a good design feature as such, add more complexity to an already complex game than I think is justified from the perspective of keeping the game "neat and clear". It might have been better as an optional variant or expansion.

RATING: 9
Four or five players.

Alterations/House rules: Poker chips in the open; Possibly Clearclaw's sell the entire route at once - Note: While one can only trade building spaces, the 'Start Anywhere' can be dragged along. Also, all deals are binding upon final agreement and the turn itself is executed atomically.

Touchstones: Everything is negotiable; Corners are valuable; One must quickly come up with a fixed price to aid bargaining; Absolute control of negotiations while it's one's turn.

This game responds to lots of bargaining, lots of propositions, lots of starting over.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
„Alkudni, tárgyalni nem lehetőség, hanem kötelesség”
Hétvégén megismerhettem a fenti játékot egy 4 személyes tanuló parti keretében.
Nem a világom a kufárkodás, kereskedés, de nem tudtam ellenállni a 3 hölgy jelentette kihívásnak (természetesen utolsó lettem, így már szívesen tudom ajánlani, hogy milyen taktikát ne használj).
Hosszú szabályértelmezések után azért „belecsaptunk a lecsóba”. Játékosszámtól függő fordulók során mindenki irányít egy-egy kört. Alapvetően „küldetéseket” kell teljesítenünk, amiért pénz szerzünk meg, valamint épületek birtokosaivá válhatunk, amiért bérleti díjat szedünk. A küldetések során több pénzért és bónusz lapkáért több terméket kell egy adott helyszínre leszállítani, vagy kevesebbet kisebb összegért. Valamikor elegendő 2 adott helyszín között lehelyezni a lépésre használt torony jelzőit. Vagy nagyobb kockázatot vállalva „rágyúrhatunk” szerződésekre (ez tényleg lutri, de ha bejön, nagy kaszálás).
A körödben dobókockával határozod meg a torony kezdőhelyszínét, amelynek 5 korongjának lerakásával mozogsz a játéktáblán (városban). Itt lépnek be a többiek a körödbe. Különböző összegekért megpróbálhatják megvenni azt, hogy merre menj a torony korongjaival. Ha elfogadod (ha, tehát nem kötelező), akkor azt betartva helyezed le a korongokat utcára vagy egy-egy épületre. Minden körben minden játékos csak egy akciót hajthat végre. A te körödben ez ingyenes, amíg másoknak legalább 5 pénzt kell fizetni érte. A te körödben a te akciódat senki nem licitálhatja felül, de a többi helyszín vonatkozásában játékostársaid addig licitálhatnak, amíg az egyikük licitjét már senki nem kínálja felül. Na itt megy a „cicaharc”, pénzköltés. Közben nem kötelező mind az 5 korongot lehelyezned, amivel akadályozni tudod, hogy az „ellenkereskedők” minél több küldetéskártyájukat teljesíthessék.
Az akciók végrehajtása után a birtokjelzőkkel rendelkező egyének az éppen aktuális játékostól kezdve 1 vagy 2 épület tulajdonjogát megszerezhetik. Amikor valaki más épületén hajt végre akciót, annak tulajdonosa a közös készletből kap 10 érmét. Egy-egy fontosabb helyszín birtoklása előnyt jelent a győzelemhez.
A játékban bónuszlapkákat is megszerezhetsz, amelyek segítségével joker árucikket vagy extra akciót kapsz, cserélhetsz bármilyen árucikket bármilyen másikra, kockadobás helyett eldöntheted, honnan kezded meg lépéseidet vagy pluszban végrehajthatod birtokolt épületed akcióját.
A játék egyik hiányosságának tartom, hogy ennél jobb szabálykönyveket is láttam már. Sok esetben hosszas kereséssel sem találtunk választ a kérdésünkre, igaz megbeszéltük és úgy játszottunk tovább.
Általában nincs ellenemre egy játékban, ha tárgyalni, alkudozni, licitálni kell, de nekem ez már túl sok (ami nem azt jelenti, hogy mások ezt a részét ne szerethetnék).
Alapvetően korrekt játék, gyors előkészítéssel és egyszerű szabályokkal, rengeteg beszéddel és kufárkodással. Akinek inge, próbálja fel. Nálam az Istanbul sokkal nyerőbb.

RATING: 6
Yeah, not as great as I first thought.  Downgraded it again (from 7 to 6) when I played it again recently, that makes two downgrades.  It just seems to be close but "not there".  An experienced player who is a bit of a talker can often convince people to make deals to their detriment (and thus doubly benefit themselves), making for an "unhappy" game where people feel fleeced or conned.  Someone who sits back and trys to collect lots of money from deals (without spending much themselves) can also do well, however this requires some good placement of your stack (either through good dice or using a lot of "pick your starting location" tiles).  One thing that can be said for this game is, there are many many ways to pull off the win!  (which is good; but i'm still not inclined to increase my rating)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a game that is all about trading.  Everything is up for trade, including ducats (which are victory points), cards in hand, special cards, goods, rights to perform the current action, and even things which will be earned as a result of taking the current action!

As a result, this game takes a long time.  In a 5-player game, there are a maximum of 35 turns, each of which involves the placement of 5 discs for at least 3-5 actions each turn.  A recent game lasted 30 turns, with 8 minutes per turn = 4 hours!

As a point of strategy, it seems that messages are by far the most efficient way to score points.  You don't have to win an action in a building to score for a message.  So, you get 30 ducats for little cost -- you don't need to pay to win an action, you don't have to collect any goods.  Compare this to 40 ducats for small orders, where you need one good and need to win an action, and 100 ducats plus a special card for large orders, where you need 3 goods from the opposite corners of the board and you need to win the action.  I think the large orders are a bad strategy -- at least with the small orders, you get to take the building's action in addition to delivering your order, whereas you can't take a privilege card if you fulfil a large order.

An effective way to play the messages strategy is to put an owner chit on the post office, and get the "building" special cards as much as possible.  Then you can trade 1:1 for a building special card, which you can then use to activate your post office and get 2 messages, which you may even be able to fulfil this turn.

Another strategy is to use 1:1 special cards early in the game to accumulate privileges.  Just don't spend too much money paying other players for their privileges -- privileges are over-valued and don't usually win the game.  Just hope that you get some contiguous ones and leave it at that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game fascinates me with its variability and its social interraction.  I like the focus on player interaction, which makes every turn and every move important.  It also various methods to victory, allowing you to modify your strategy successfully during play.

The down-side to this game is best described as the difference between an trading game and a negotiation game.  If you focus on good trades, the game can go quickly.  If your group has to negotiate every trade to get the most $5 possible, it can drag out.  For these groups, I would advocate a 3 min. timer.  

While the mechanisms are not difficult, the game has significant numbers of possible actions that must be understood, so players need to be good at moderately complicated rules and a certain amount of ambiguity in play.

RATING: 7
Very clever. I'd somehow expected a little more tension in the negotiations, but I guess this is at least partly a side effect of playing with children.  Knowing the value of the different buildings is quite difficult--at first, at least, it was very easy for me to be far more focused on what I had in my hand rather than what else I might gain on the board. The "start any space" tokens are extremely valuable. I do like the limiting factor of the tower, and it seems like knowing WHEN to negotiate is also a very important part of the game. Is there any way to influence where the tower goes, when you want the third or fourth thing it might hit?

I was kind of disappointed to find I'd won--it makes me think less of the game because I didn't win too many actions, so perhaps they aren't as valuable as they should be.  (1 play)

RATING: 7
Played this on June 23rd for the 2nd time.  I didn't seem to like this much years ago for the first play, or at least it being memorable.  Having said that, it was a great gameplay this go around.  There is good luck/strategy balance.  We were watching our 10 month old while playing so it seemed long, however I think it's pretty short (as a 4 player game) perhaps an hour w/o distractions.  It doest take some time to learn, perhaps 15 minutes w/o the distractions.  It's worth picking up for a game play. I'd highly recommend this for a game play.  Chances are it will work into your rotation of games to play every once in a while.  Update: my 3rd game gave me a bad taste for the game. RATING: 3
Traders is a textbook case of how a negotiation game SHOULDN'T be made.  There is too much hidden information to evaluate decisions and the values of items, too many busy mechanics that don't add anything of substance to the design, and too much restriction on how to go about executing a goal.  Add to the fact, it is way too long and entirely too abstracted.  I mean giant "trading towers" that collapse orthagonally around a town executing auctions that must be made if an offer is bid?  WTF?  This is one of those games that is supposed to wow people with it's "depth" and "elegance," but in my mind it is completely without both.

RATING: 6
2012-02-09

Initial Rating: 8.5 (October 2011)

I love negotiation games so I really like Genoa, one of the best negotiations games I have played. Everything, and I mean EVERYTHING in the game is up for trade which is a nice feature but this can cause some people to take very long turns. 

What sets Genoa apart from other negotiation games (like Chinatown which I enjoy) is the hidden information, in Genoa you are not sure how much value holds many of the deals until the end of the game so you need to calculate carefully to succeed in this game and this makes the game a lot more complex. I LOVE IT. 

Another good feature is that you have different paths to victory, unlike many negotiation games. The only down (for some people) is the long game time, it can be easy over 3 hours. If you like negotiation games this is one of the best.

Bottom line, Genoa is a blast but only with the right group as this one is very group dependent.  Try it before buy it.

RATING: 9
This is my favourite game in the alea 'big box' line.  Definitely my favourite trading game, and one of the best 'hose your neighbour' games of all time.  The 120+ minute play time is the only thing that keeps it from being rated higher.  If you like deal making... this game is for you.

Everything in the game is tradeable!  All cards, tokens, etc. can be worked into any deal you can think of.  A big plus is that you can play it Active... "I want 'x' to move here, or I'm ending my turn!" or Passive... "How much am I offered to go here?"

Tower movement is key to the game.   And, with hidden money, you are never sure who is ahead... a nice plus for everyone with a poor memory like mine.

RATING: 7
Interesting game.  People who like the trading aspect of Settlers of Catan should also like Traders of Genoa.  In ToG, you trade money, cards, and/or goods (basically anything in the game) to be able to take actions during another players turn.  This means that when it's your turn, you're trying to earn a pretty good payout. Determining WHERE to move your 'workers' is important to ensure a bidding war.  There are also multiple tracks to earn money (big orders, small orders, messages, privilieges, owner tokens).  The 120 minute play time overstays its welcome a bit.  But each player's turn is interactive in that players are bidding on where to move the 'workers'  so it goes by without much downtime.

I still prefer Settlers (especially with the Cities and Knights expansion).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I have to say this much for the game, it's pretty unique. The game board is a map of the town center, with streets and buildings, placed on an 8x8 grid. A typical player's turn consists of taking control of the "tower" (a stack of 5 disks), rolling two 8-sided dice to see where in the town the tower is initially placed, and then moving the tower to adjacent spaces, leaving behind one disk in each space, making a trail of disks across the grid. When the tower is placed in a building, the action associated with that building usually takes place. After the tower is finished spreading across town, the turn ends and the tower is given to the next player.

What's unique? Every single move the tower makes can be negotiated with the player controlling the tower. The controlling player can only take one of the five possible actions the tower placement allows, so the rest can be sold off to the highest bidder. Players use money, goods, ownership tiles, order cards, special actions cards, and non-binding promises in order to influence the active player to give the next action to them. That's right. EVERY action taken in the game is open to negotiation. The object is to finish with the most money. Money is made primarily by meeting certain objectives on your hand of order cards (large orders, small orders and messages). You can also place ownership tiles on buildings which gives you some money when someone else uses that action and gives you a bit of money at the end of the game. There are also privilege cards with buildings on them that you can collect, hoping to build sets of adjacent buildings for end game bonuses.

The heart of the game are the negotiations that get you the things you need to make money. If you don't like negotiations, you will HATE this game. If negotiations are your favorite aspect of gaming, you will likely love it. Fans of this game say that the longer the game takes, the better it is, because that means the negotiations are getting difficult and complex. 

When we played we didn't get very creative with our negotiations and the game dragged, taking on a repetitive, samey quality. It also didn't help that I basically won the game by rolling the perfect placement on my final turn, enabling me to complete a large order without paying, and winning by $15.

RATING: 7
I still think this is one of the better trading/negotiation games out there but I find negotiation games difficult to get to the table and so off it goes to someone who might play more often!

FIRST IMPRESSIONS:

Wow!  This has got to be the best negotiation/trading game out there.  The all encompassing aspects of being able to trade practically anything and with many different ways to earn money as well is almost intimidating on the first play.  Still not sure value wise what everything is worth...and since its often worth more to someone else, makes life even more difficult.  Word to the wise - don't let anyone get too many privilege cards, those can add up in a hurry.  Also, be prepared to be sitting for quite a while, particularly on your first play as you seek to figure out the many varied nauances to the game.  Just remember, whoever has the most money at the end of the game wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Robust, engrossing experience.  The system is front-loaded with a number of rules that serve to ratchet down the system and ensure it doesn't devolve into a chaotic, Pit-style bazaar.  The trick is to make the most creative, beneficial deals within the rules straightjacket.  The worth of ALL wares and cards are constantly changing throughout the game and must be re-evaluated according to the in-game situation.  As in real economics, the greatest assest in this game is OPPORTUNITY.

My concern is that in a game between equally shrewd players, the victory will be handed to the player who finds the last significant windfalls in the form of Small Orders he can already fill with the goods in his supply, or Messages he can deliver in a chain, or indeed to the player who got the best Privilege cards.  If my suspicion is correct, ToG can almost be said to be an "experience game".  But what an experience!

RATING: 8
This is the very best negotiation game every made.  The fundamental mechanic is how the actions tower moves.  You negotiate for additional actions each round. There are myriad of different building each providing resources, contracts, special cards, building control, etc.  

You have to have a rule thought that negotiations for any given move can only be 30-60 sec.  If you cannot make a quick deal you move the tower.  If people are going to argue over every little deal, the game will take forever.  

The other difficulty with the game is people cannot always determine the value for some of the goods.   You need to tell players about the possible value associated with the large contract buildings.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Pure negotiation game typically clocking in at 1-3 hours with 3 hours not being uncommon (although rated at 120 min and can be played as short as 45 min). A LOT of begging, convincing, and other forms of negotiation is at play here. One reviewer mentioned that he has played games going as long as 5 hours!!!

Reviews have been outstanding. Contrast to QUO VADIS, a tighter and lighter pure negotiation game w/quicker play, where you know where you stand before the end of the game.

Although it pales in comparison, CHINATOWN does have some negotiation and plays much faster. It would seem to be much more suited to casual games and, as such, would likely see it's way to the gaming table more often than ToG. It also incorporates a Settlers of Catan and Acquire element into the theme (i.e., acquiring properties that have value and produce goods - I think?); which is not necessarily a positive point if one is looking for a pure negotiation game. (In such a case, that's where one would look towards QUO VADIS as a ToG substitute).

It's been suggested to play with open money and scoring.

NOT a game for newcomers or folks younger than the stated 12 y/o.

This review makes the game a MUST have 
(Trade anything!! Yet the 3 hour time is a killer. Even 2 is tough to justify owning. Perhaps just find a game club): http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/347446/top-10-reasons-why-traders-of-genoa-is-in-my-top-1

Can be passed off as a family game, but requires the right family: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/136626/is-traders-of-genoa-a-family-game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After 12 years this game is still the ultimate trading game. Everything can be traded with minimal set of rules makes this game simply the best in the genre. The pricing system has some sort of autoregulatory mechanism so the price would change as the board position change. This is not easily recognised by new players, though.
Tha downsides?
1. It may be exceptionally long esp played with new-gamers;
2. In contrary to most modern europe designs where superior strategy is going to win the game, this game has some similiarity to party games in which communication plays a vital role in both enjoying and winning the game.

RATING: 9
The best part of this game is that all comodities can be traded with -- not only wares and money, but also messages, orders, privileges, owner markers, and special cards. And with the 1:1 special card you don't even need another player!

Furthermore, there is virtually no downtime, constant player interaction, no leader advantage or disadvantage(!) and there are lots of tactical and even some strategical decisions to be made.

"If you go to the postoffice and allow me to make my action, then I'll give you one of the messages..."
"And I let you even pick which one..."
"OK, thank you, then I use this 1:1 card to exchange this silk for a building action card, which I then use to pick up a salt and a pepper, because I have an owner marker there. Oh, and I use the salt to deliver this small order to the postoffice!"
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Un juego en que cada movimiento es una transacción; debido a eso, puede durar más tiempo del normal. REGALADO a Paulo.
Puede gustar a quienes el regateo y el negociado es su fuerte. El que esté más atento a negociar (tras una estrategia básica predefinida), va a ganar.
Las vías iniciales para ganar son muchas; pero es en la negociación y en determinar la ganancia que cada negociación implica (costos menos valor de transacción), donde se decide quien termina con más dinero, que es el objetivo del juego.

El juego se lo regalé a mi hermano, para su matrimonio.
UPDATE: mayo 2005....me da lata jugarlo, porque obliga, igual que Los Colonos de Catán, a estar regateando el movimiento de la torre en cada segundo del juego. Prefiero los juegos más sosegados y no con tanta negociación.

RATING: 8
Interesting game with a lot of different strategies to explore, and the need to stay always on your toes to react to what other people are doing. Highly interactive and in fact more negotiation-based that I was expecting: the rules suggested there was a spatial element to this that didn't really come across in the game. Seems to run a little long because the endgame falls a bit flat - everyone can see the end coming and so is usually just tying things up by that stage. Amusingly it also offers a mini-lesson in the advantages and pitfalls of capitalism. The biggest issue with the game is that experienced players can start to make quite accurate judgements about the value of given trades, which makes it more mathematical and less intuitive to play.

RATING: 7
I dropped this down to an 8. It doesn't work as well, it seems, as an ending to 4 to 6 hours of gaming. The last few rounds seemed to drag even though it was only about an hour and a half in. The whole game took almost two hours, which isn't that long since two players were new. I enjoyed the game, but I had the idea that few others were ready to call it quits. It seems the game starts pretty strong but if the tower doesn't start at the market (or if a player doesn't hurry the game by choosing that), there is a lull at about round 5. The end scoring (if not hurried by a closing coffee shop) is not bad and typical of many "euros."

I wish I could find people that really enjoy the game because I really like to play this one very competitively. Or maybe that's the problem :) .

RATING: 9
Bought this one based on positive reviews.  Reading the rules for the first time, I was immediately intrigued by the tower movement/negotiation mechanic and could not wait to give it a play.  The mechanics work just as well in practice as they sound on paper. First play was very enjoyable -- lots of animated, but not prolonged negotiation. We were perhaps bidding a little higher than we should have been, and the player who bid the least ended up winning.  Second play was a little more subdued -- perhaps bidding lower than true values, and the player who bid the most (me) won.  Look forward to more plays to better learn how much to offer.  Another one of those games where you want to do a lot more than you can, leading to some interesting decisions.

RATING: 7
Traded away the original Rio Grande printing. Now have a copy of the recent edition.

A tight, tense experience with the right group. As with all negotiation & trading games, the flexibility in the rules allows for some interesting situations - an economy and valuation that mutates over the course of the game. It must ABSOLUTELY be played in the right spirit - otherwise stupid crap like kingmaking can occur which defeats the whole point of the game.

This game is doing something right.

RATING: N/A
This is a game that is a little on the heavy side, but it is all player interaction, negotiation, and is tight. 

You are trying to generate profit by trading with other players in a city, delivering goods, obtaining favors, and sending letters from one place to another.

Money is kept secret and the gameplay is subtle. Gameplay is 60-120 mins, and it would probably be closer to 120 for us.

Would need to play with the right group.

RATING: 8.5
The more I play this game the more I like it. It's very impressive to realize that you can be very creative during the negotiations and come up with deals that are mutually (if not equally) profitable with both of the trading players. And the more deals you manage to make the more you increase your possibilities for a good result of even the victory in the end. Also a big plus of the game is that the setup is very easy and doesn't take too much time. The rules are rather easy to grasp once you have seen them in an example of play. So, when playing with new players I usually play one round with them so that they learn the rules and then we start the normal game.

RATING: 4
I don't normally say this about many games, but Genoa feels obsolete. There are so many other games that do what this game sets out to accomplish in better ways. This game feels very slow-paced, dated, and a little too equal in how everything is distributed. For me, Tiny Epic Kingdoms scratches a lot of the same itches, even though it's quite a bit different in its approach. Unless you're new to the world of Euro games, there's not a lot to see here that hasn't already been improved elsewhere. Even then, I'd rather point people to other more recent games.

Would rather play Tiny Epic Kingdoms.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Traders of Genoa is the best negotiation game I've played, and for that it deserves to be in just about anyone's collection.  The multiple paths to victory, the interaction of all the mechanics, everything works together really well.  There are tough decisions to be made, and then you have to learn the tendencies of your opponents and try to convince them to do what's best for you.

Where this game will succeed or fail for a group is the type of players in the group.  More than anything, this is a game that is dependant upon the group for success.  People have to be willing to negotiate in good faith and not take anything personally when it doesn't work out.  With people who know what they are doing and are into the negotiations, this game is great.  But without the right people, it will suffer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Ultimately, rather disappointing. I was sold this game as a 'step up on Chinatown', but in fact it's nothing of the sort. In essence, it's too long and too fiddly, the player interaction feels woefully forced, and the economy and frivolous 'does this take an action or not'-type exchanges are flawed. I would take Chinatown every time over this.

I deeply love negotiation games, and I deeply love Rudiger Dorn games, and for these two frivolous reasons only is this not rated a 4.

[i]Traded as part of bundle for British Rails, Mondo, Through The Desert and El Grande[/i]

RATING: N/A
Fun, medium weight and quick weeknight game to play! I wish we had pulled this one out to play sooner! I really find this game to be easy to learn, teach, and remember the rules for play! It is a great 2-player experience and I can't wait to try it with more players...the game should be even better I suspect! Genoa's ancestor might be Puerto Rico and it might even be the predecessor to cooperative games! The game play almost feels cooperative in bargaining with the Tower player! I really enjoy trying to convince the Tower player to travel to places I want to go...sweetening the deal with goods and money! The game does not feel cutthroat at all and makes for a great two player game!

RATING: N/A
5/01,
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
心得:
兩人應該玩不出嘴砲的精隨
遊戲主要是收集物品完成訂單或是完成特定路線計分卡
放置圓片路徑應該是遊戲的特色
真的要嘴砲應該蠻熱鬧的遊戲
規則:
*回合標記根據人數放圖版左邊對應位置，五種特殊版塊，8個貨物放在圖版標示的各個建築上，卡牌分類洗勻也放在對應的位置上
*每個玩家選一個顏色放對應的擁有者六腳柱在圖版對應位置，拿一個對應顏色的板塊記錄你的顏色，每種卡每個玩家抽一張跟130元(錢是隱藏資訊)，隨機起始拿起始標記
*遊戲根據玩家人數進行不同輪數，每輪每個玩家會輪流擔任主動玩家一個回合後該輪結束，一個主動玩家回合分成四個階段，該輪結束回合標記下移，如果回合標記移動時在最後一格以下遊戲結束計分
1.主動玩家丟兩顆色，決定五個疊起的行動原片起始座標
2.移動，行動，談判  此階段主動玩家決定如何移動起始原片，每個建築物或街道格算一格，每次移動只能到相鄰沒原片的地點，且要留一塊在原地點，最多四步可不移動，移動到建築可執行行動，此階段每個玩家只能執行一次行動，所以藉由談判給予任意資源(錢，物品，卡片，特殊版塊，擁有標記等)來執行行動
*談判時要遵守，如果走到建築物後且以人提供交易品，你如果不能執行就一定要答應，交易時不能用未來行動來交易，但可以用該行動獲得的東西交易
3.獲取建築物 照主動玩家開始順位，每人可以選擇一個在街道的原片放一個擁有者標記在相鄰的建築物上(相鄰兩個時可放兩個)，放完原片移除，之後換人
*如果要放的建築物有標記，可支付一個標記移除之，之後在放，一個原片相鄰兩個建築可一次放兩個
*遊戲中只要其他人(你自己不算)在你有標記的建築行動你就從銀行拿10元，遊戲最後也會拿10元
4.回合結束 移動回合標記向下一格
*遊戲結束 每個場上擁有者標記拿10元，然後展示契約卡拿對應金錢，錢最多的贏，平手比剩下的貨物特殊版塊跟手牌和
*遊戲中手牌無上限，都是隱藏的
***2人特殊規則***
*每次交易一定要提供一種物品(貨物特殊版塊或卡片)，不能只有錢
*主動玩家可執行兩個建築物行動，如果另一個玩家有做一個行動的話
***建築物行動列表***
>Guild hall 抽1大訂單卡，必須使用對應villa跟對應3貨物來完成獲得100元
>city hall/post office  抽兩張對應的卡片
*小訂單只要玩家在上面對應的建築執行行動時，就可以返還貨品來完成拿40元(一次行動只能送一筆)，信差卡要此回合移動圓片有在對應個兩個建築物上可馬上打出完成(不用你是主動玩家)
>cathedral 拿兩個擁有者標記
>特殊版塊區  拿一個特殊版塊放面前
>倉庫區 兩種貨物各拿一個放面前
>Villas 抽一張契約卡或是完成大合約
*契約卡根據上面的建築物是否相鄰拿對應金額，越多相鄰越多錢
>中央市場  每有行動，但是如果起始位置在這裡則回合軌向下移動一格
**特殊行動版塊 拿到後可選擇什麼時候使用
>任意起始位置 當你要丟骰時可改成放到指定位置
>額外行動 在執行行動階段時可丟棄之，此回合行動上限數+1
>任意貨物 在自己行動前中後可使用拿任意一個貨物
>建築行動 使用時機同上，可馬上使用有你標記的建築物行動，不能用來完成小訂單，不算在此回合行動上限內
>交易 時機同上，可1換1(特殊卡、貨物指示物、所有人指示物及遊戲卡)

RATING: 7
The game promotes some dull and frustrating moments along the way when you have little interest in the current stalled negotiations - although there's always the fun option of table talking the current tower mover out of doing something good for someone else.

The whole game is talk, talk, talk ... talk, talk, talk. There is no drama, tension, storyline, advancement or progression. Just one negotiation after another - and trying to make each one count. I don't mind this as a change, but it's also not something I want each gaming session either.

The mid-game provides some more interesting trades involving commodities, cards and special action chits. Thef aster it is, the better it is. Decisiveness, gentleman, that is the name of the game! Tempered by the fear of losing out on a possible win-win.

RATING: 7.5
I'm slowly reading the rules. What should I say about a game that I own since months and I couldn't play because I could only recently get translated rules, and dozens of pages of german rules are too much? TOO DIFFICULT! Why are games so difficult? It even has too many german-language pieces... Actually reading the rules the game seems nice, so I suspend my negative vote until when I'll play my first game in 2037, when I will find the time to teach it to someone. The real reason for which I bought this game is that... I'M FROM GENOA!!
==== Update ==== I took about 2 years to read the rules but finally I did it :-) and it is not difficult as it seems when trying to understand all those pages. It is quick enough, with a very complex strategy and it gives you the chance to decide your own way to victory or to be transported for a decent match without any decisive choice. Very good. It is probably one of the most interactive games ever, together with Siedler, although it increases only after some games, when negotiation becomes smart enough.
ONE NOTE: The spirit of the commercial town is well depicted, but Genoa doesn't have parks, and the historic center is famous not to have almost any square. There are lots of palaces, instead, a nice cathedral (but completely different from the one depicted): in Genoa marble and ardesia (a black stone) are used to build buildings and churchs with horizontal stripes. And there are no "streets", rather very dark and tight pathways to the sea! 8-)

RATING: 8.3
We had our first play of this last night.  WOW.  This is the perfect game for our gaming group.  I really wish we'd grabbed this one years ago. RATING: 4
UPDATE Sept. 2011: Dropped from a 6 to a 4. Essentially a worker placement game in which the active player (a) chooses which jobs/roles/actions are available and (b) chooses which players get which actions. Of course, (a) and (b) are all open to negotiation.

That itself is very clever. What really annoys be about this game is that the game could *break* if only some players start keeping track of how much money each player has throughout the game. If one or two players are doing that, then the others are bound to lose.

Perhaps this game could be radically improved if money is open knowledge. In that case, players will start making more inventive trades with goods and special power cards, both of which are more difficult to put a value on.

I prefer negotiation games (such as Diplomacy) where it is impossible to put a numerical value on a deal. (Too bad Diplomacy takes too long to play). Or at least games that have long term goals (rather than short-term goals in Genoa).

This game stays in my collection because my lawyer friends really enjoy it.

May 2007: Awesome negotigation game ... that I can only tolerate once a year. There is one huge problem with the game. If the game goes by quickly, then it sucks because players haven't been negotigating much and have probably relied on luck or poor bluffing skills to achieve their contracts.

However, once people figure out how negotiations really work, then the game really shines. However, then the game takes *forever*.

I actually prefer Chinatown or I'm the Boss over this one; Traders of Genoa has a lot of fiddly rules compared to these two.

RATING: 6
Things turned nasty in the third game when I discovered this is another solo-itis game. You just don't seem to be able to gain sufficient knowledge into what other players are going to do, so you concentrate on your own strategy. This is fine, as it works more or less in PoF and Elfenland. The trouble is after an hour and a half I couldn't be bothered haggling to have the trading tower favourably placed. I never felt I'd worked hard to deliever the large/small orders either, it felt more like luck. 

The worst problem, however, is it is just too long. I've never had to negotiate so often in my life. I played again after eight years (!) and while the game is clean, well designed and a typically taut euro, the repetitive negotiating made me lose interest by round 4. This a once yearly game for sure.

RATING: 7
About half way through the game I decided that it was more beneficial for me to do less in Genoa than to do more.  I think that it's a bad sign in a game when I find it more useful to just sit there.  But for some reason, I didn't feel that way for most of the game of Genoa.  I think it was my enjoyment of the nervous tension of wondering what each player would do and wondering if maybe there would be a way for me to try to get in on some of the money trading fun.

I did enjoy the nervous tension as well as the ability to plan to attempt to get the most out each turn.  I found that the starting spots I rolled were mostly worthless, so I had a really good time attempting to sell my turns to other people and I think that selling as many of your turns as you can is really a winning strategy.  But I guess it's hard to determine a winning strategy after just one play.

RATING: 9
A game which demands constant attention and creativity. To win, you need to make quick tactical decisions about what you want out of each move of the stack, and then need to make creative offers that will appropriately entice your opponents, but not give them undue reward. Oddly similar to (but far less constrained than) Modern Art, where the group's mindset and how to make different offers to different players are both influential.

RATING: 6
My first game was slightly marred by having a player at the table who thought other players should only make moves they agreed with. This game is a fairly standard delivery game in which players earn money for collecting and delivering goods around the table, the objective being to end with the most money. 
The twist is that during a players turn up to five actions can be taken but the player can only take one action themselves. They can offer the other actions to other players in order to gain extra money. The game is a little too complex around this central theme, which is a pity because the central idea works very well in other games like Santiago.

alea
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great negotiation/trading game.  Less AP prone than some other economic games because actions are usually either very easy or pretty difficult to calculate exactly how much something is worth to you.  But negotiations can still really bog the game down if you're not involved in them.  My main complaint is that the game, instead of escalating with too many good options to choose from near the end like many point-salad Euros, instead you have diminishing options that will be profitable, with most options worthless to you.  I think the game would benefit from reduced number of rounds and removal of the random round movement when the tower goes to the center spot 1/9 chance; that would make it easier to calculate exactly how many rounds you have left and I think speed up the final turns.

RATING: 6
Decent game with real interaction between players.  Players who complain about getting screwed in games should steer clear.  In ToG, you only get screwed if you dont know how to haggle with a positive result in mind. This one is a bit too long for what it is.  With a lot of fiddly negotiations, you could be sitting there twiddling your thumbs.

Keep this away from players prone to Analysis Paralysis...  it's nearly insufferable.

Make sure that you read the rules concerning the 1:1 trades and set the board up as shown in the rules...  all cards, wares, 1:1 tiles, and owner markers are tradable with the 1:1.

RATING: 9
After my first game,I thought that Traders was everything a game should be. After the second game, I still pretty much think that, but with a condition. This, more than any other game I have ever played, is a game whose enjoyment is affected by the group you play with. If everyone goes into the game in the mood to make quick deals and have a good time, it's fantastic. If the players want to labor over every decision, it's not so fun. I totally disagree with the comments that this is a "gamer" game. I think that taking it too seriously and coming from a "gamer" approach takes a lot away from it. Likewise, the length of the game (oft complained about) depends on the players, not the mechanics. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A rich, complex game with some unique mechanics. Lots of player interaction. You bid with your money to affect available actions, but must do so carefully because money is the victory condition.
