RATING: 7
Rating is for the 2-4 player games -- haven't played with 5 yet. It's actually pretty good with two, which is unusual for a trade / negotiation game.

The cool thing about this game is how open it is. You are free to trade pretty much anything (not only spices and whatnot, but money, cards, and even fractions of things you are about to receive). On top of this, you can use certain cards to string together long profitable combos of cube shuffling. I kept looking in the rulebook thinking, "surely that's not allowed!", but it all is, and explicitly so in a brain-melting example of play.

The teeny little board means that I can play it on my coffee table without leaving my gargantuan couch.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game has a rather clever mechanic which limits the number of moves you can make per turn, and randomizes where you start on a 8x8 grid.  Cards in your hand give you a goal which, upon completion, earn you money; at the end of the game the player with the most money wins.  Each turn each player has opportunity for one action, but usually you want more than one.  Actions include things such as picking up merchandise, gaining special abilities, and delivering merchandise.  Only one player gets to chose the which actions will be taken by moving a set of markers (the merchant) up to 5 spaces on the board; players may negotiate and offer deals to get the merchant to go to a spot on the board that has an action they want.  This can lead to a bidding war, and the player controlling the merchant can refuse all offers, however once an offer is accepted both parties are bound by it.

Having only one action available each turn (there is a special tile that will grant an extra action, but it takes an action to get this tile) causes players to carefully weigh what they want each turn, and how much they are willing to trade for it.  The game does not lag, even when not controlling the merchant, since each player who has not taken an action still has opportunity to negotiate for an action.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A great trading game, with very high and only positive interaction. This genre requires the values of traded goods to be difficult to calculate and different for other players to make for difficult decisions. To some extent it is true here: it certainly is not obvious how much you should be willing to pay for what you want in order to still make profit, but there were some attempts to solve it mathematically. If you are a math geek and must play optimally, the game will lose some appeal. For more casual gamers just going by their intuition (which is a valid approach) it's a great experience. If you liked the interaction in Settlers of Catan, get this by all means.

RATING: 8
Played twice (initial rating). Wow, this is a substantial game, I am very impressed. Basically this is a 3 hour bargaining on the market which will completely drain all the players.  First game to do that to me since Le Havre, but where Le Havre feels like 3 hour solitary mall shopping this is SO much more satisfying. Still it's a bit long for what it.. Also I admire the game for being a very open design -  the players will define the feeling of the game and you must adapt. Some strategies will not ork with another group of people. I prefer to play it in a cuthroat way. Not for introverts and not for people who get offended easily. This is all out bargaining, shut up or hold a grudge and you'll lose. Also not for new gamers (I might looking a for lighter and shorter negotiating game to fill that niche). I raised the rating half a point for being such an intriguing design - something to admire (maybe even more than play). An artwork of interpersonal protocols. 
 
Overpaid philosophia edition of the game. 60€ is just damn crazy for the huge empty box you get. Still the game being eye-candy made it easier to get to the table. Also the chits are for me improvement over cubes and the coins are also better than papermoney. 

edit: needs the right kind of people to work - the loud people.

RATING: 10
[3 plays] 5 players and 4. This sat for a long while until we finally had the right group at the right time. I've owned it patiently for 3 years, nominated it before, but finally got it played. WOW, was it ever worth it. This is hands down the best negotiation game I have ever experienced. There are so many routes to victory, so many tactical options to consider, and just so much tension at times. Everything about this game is terrific, including the theme, artwork, and components.

I didn't think I had a guaranteed victory in the bag, but at the end, I was pretty confident that I would finish close to the top. So did most if not all of the other players. I did, in fact, finish basically tied for last. Everyone was surprised by the overall results. All 3 of the top players had pursued various strategies to victory. My mistakes had been ignorance of how much to pay in some negotiations and completely ignoring the value of privilege cards, which made them even more valuable for those collecting them.

Anyway, it was among the best 3 hours of gaming in which I've been involved in many years. I LOVED it! I will play this anytime and anywhere there are enthusiastic players who take the game seriously and take the tough negotiations with a pleasant attitude.

UPDATE: Loving it just as much after the 3rd play.


Most recent play 10-7-11 with Donovan, Carter, and Corey.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of the best games of negotiation: EVERYTHING is up for dealing. 

The board shows a town full of buildings, each allowing a different type of action. The choice of available actions each turn is very cleverly chosen: each turn, the player rolls dice to randomly place a column of 5 discs. Then, he moves the column space by space as far as he wishes, leaving 1 disc behind in each space. At each building space, the building action may be used by a single player who has not already taken an action this turn; it goes to whoever makes the best deal with the person taking his turn! Actions include a variety of things like production of goods, orders for goods, special action tokens, etc. Whoever has the most money at the end of the game wins!


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is a very good game but definitely one that falls in that stereotype of "too long for what it is".

The game is a trading and negotiation game where players collect different types of goods in order to fulfill orders and make money.  The action mechanism is pretty unique in that on a player's turn he makes up to 5 actions available but can only take one of them himself.  The rest are sold off to the highest bidder.  That gives this game a great deal of depth and interaction with lots of interesting choices to be made.

It's not terribly complicated and my first game of this took upwards of 3 hours which is too long for a game that's just about resource trading and order delivery.  That being said I don't think it needed to take that long.  It took over an hour longer than the listed time on BGG, which while not always accurate, seems much closer to what it should be.  We didn't even play with the maximum number of players.

I think to play this game you need a person to take charge and hurry the pace along or outright institute a timer for negotiations, which is where the lion's share of the time took.

I would definitely play this game again but I don't want it to take that long and I know the people I play with wouldn't.

RATING: 7
The newer, re-released version of the game is the one that I've played.  And I was quite impressed with it, even if I find myself waffling on the "fun factor".  In my gaming experience, this is truly a unique game (it is certainly unlike any game I've played before), which is centered around a "negotiation" mechanic.  This means that the game experience is going to depend heavily on the specific group with which you are playing - people who are very serious/easily offended aren't likely to do well with this one.  But for those of us who like to have fun with our games, it certainly creates some interesting and entertaining interaction between players.  I'm just not so sure about having the negotiation aspect as the central mechanic since I generally dislike negotiation in games, although there are a couple of exceptions ("Bootleggers"!).  Over-all, I enjoyed my first play and would play it again, but I'm not sure if it would have long-term replay potential for myself.  And a quick note on the physical components: the new edition looks very nice - the board is gorgeous, the money tokens are easy to handle, and the rest of the bits and cards are functional.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3/5  Total: 7/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I'm used to games where the height of player-to-player interaction is an auction. Oh, we enjoy table talk, but the direct interactions required for the game itself are limited and narrow in scope.

Traders of Genoa is completely different. I don't know how to handle it; certainly I'm awful at it. The wheeling-and-dealing in the likes of Modern Art is fun, but in Traders it's at another level entirely. You [i]must[/i] trade. You must negotiate. Any time you want something, you can expect to have to bribe someone into letting you take it.

Oh, there's a hefty luck factor -- it's entirely possible to have a large order dropped right into your lap -- but I don't think it's ever likely to be a determining factor, and that's an important distinction. Nothing in Traders is useless because everything can be traded. You make your own opportunities.

Two hours of constant negotiation is somewhere close to my idea of hell, but it does make Traders one of the most engaging games I've ever played, and a far more intense experience than I expect from a euro.

You might hate it, but you've got to try it at least once.

RATING: 4
Where to begin... the theme is weird with this "falling tower" on the city, but I don't play games for theme -- I play them more for mechanisms.

This game fails there as well. Unless you play this game A LOT and know the value of everything, it is almost impossible to understand what to offer teammates for each thing. Since the game is almost entirely negotiation based, there is a huge kingmaker problem present. Not only that, but the dice roll for where you can start to choose your actions combined with what cards you draw during the game really make it a very random and chaotic mess.

Maybe if they removed the randomness from where the tower started (and changed the special card of "determine where you start" to compensate) and made some of the card less random it might be more strategic, but I find a lot of the areas really random. If you happen to get some good trades that are in the same area with messages, you just do insanely better than someone who had been planning from the start.

Overall, I don't see the need to play this game again. I got bored and lost focus about 1/4th the way through the game -- and it lasted nearly 3 hours. I'd much rather play something like Chinatown or even Settlers of Catan for negotiation. At least there I feel like I can control the randomness of the resources by using statistical probability. Here everything is just random.

RATING: 6.5
This is one of those games where I think using the BGG "recommended" ratings would be an injustice.  The Traders of Genoa is a fascinating game, where anything and everything has value, and even abstract things like "Start any space" pieces can be bought, sold, and traded for concrete things like metals or fabrics.  However, even one player that isn't "into" the game can destroy it for the other players, and this is a game where everyone must really want to get involved - wheedle, trade, and make a deal - for the game to be fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
What a great game this is! We just played our first game of it, with four players. Despite all the crazy wheeling and dealing that ensued, we all ended up within 25 points of each other at the end - with the leader only 5 points ahead!
Everything about this game strikes the right chord. There are some interesting mechanics in the moving of the tower, and the general mechanics of scoring are sufficiently varied to provide interest, but undoubtedly the star of the game is the trading. Being able to trade every in-game item between players raises the game from good to brilliant. The negotiation of deals between the players is so much fun. On top of all that, the theme works, the artwork is beautiful, and the components are high quality.
I'm happy to call this my new favourite game after only one play, and I can't wait to give it another go!

Edit: Okay, perhaps predictably, this didn't grab me anywhere near as much on our second game. I've lowered my score by nearly a whole point, and I'm giving it the benefit of the doubt by not taking it even lower. It didn't help that the tactics I decided to pursue this time around were the ones that were aggressively blocked by the other players, while the player who was blatantly obviously in the lead was left to his own devices, and hence won by a comfortable margin. The game thus devolves into merely trying to convince the other players that you're not winning; that seems a bit boring to me.

RATING: 7.5
This is a great negotiation game. I've had a chance to play with 3 players, and it was brilliant. Constant deals were thrown about the table to influence the controlling player to move to certain locations. The meta-game found in Genoa is fascinating. My prediction is that the most charismatic negotiator will usually win, and that is an interesting social exercise. Some would argue that a social experiment does not make a boardgame, but I don't care. It is a game that does a good job at simulating traders cutting deals and negotiating with each other. I am a big fan.

RATING: 10
Sublimely unique.  I love the idea that EVERYTHING is tradable.  A Euro I can really get behind, with lots of heavy negotiation and bluffing, and very little dry cube crunching. In my opinion, the more players the better (despite increased playtime), because then actions start to go a lot closer to their REAL worth as there's more competition.  

It can run longer than expected, and it is really tough to figure out how much to pay in your first couple games.  Things tend to settle in at a fixed price as a game goes on, which means that getting bargains can be pretty tough.  Lots of room for sneakiness in other ways, such as getting your messages and small orders delivered.

RATING: 7
This game is extremely group dependent- with people who don't like to haggle this will flop miserably, as you have to negotiate for even the right to take an action. Conversely, some of the best gaming experiences of my life have been playing this game with friends who weren't even gamers. Unlike most negotiation games I've played where it's easy to evaluate what an item is worth because the mechanics are so simple and/or there is only one currency that can be traded, here there are enough different currencies and enough hidden info that valuation is very difficult, which opens the door to some creative dealmaking. So to make a long story short- if you've got a group of people who enjoys negotiating this is a must-buy otherwise it's a definite pass
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4

They seem too free-form and unstructured for me to feel like I have a lot of control over them, and it's also very difficult for me to value what something is worth to me, much less what something will be worth to other players in the game.  This might be the finest negotiation game ever made (and judging by its ranking, it's at least among the top five), but it's a negotiation game, so I'm unlikely to want to play it very often.  Bleah.

RATING: 4
Some negotiation (bartering, nagging, begging) games push the right buttons for me.  Traders of Genoa is not one of those games.

This one makes me want to completely stop talking just so the game will end more quickly.  Unless I activate my bionic mouth and bionic patience, 60 minutes is all that I can stand of constant wheedling.  ToG is longer than that by a factor of two.

RATING: 9
A very different game. Everything is up for negotiation, which means it can take ages to play with people who can never reach an agreement. Full of options and the tower movement is brilliant. You have to spend money to make money and you win by having the most money at the end. That's something I usually dislike, but it works here.

Genoa has all this weird and specific rules about purchasing buildings and when and how you should trade with other players... Idk, I need to play this again anyway.

RATING: 7
I don't think you can like trading/negotiation games and not like this.  Everything is up for barter... your goods, your cards, where to move your pieces... Add in hidden objectives in the cards to make money and you get a very fun uncertainty as to the value of your various trades.  Seems like it might run a bit long based on one play... if it tightens up a bit this rating will rise.

Update: When we want to just trade stuff we always reach for Chinatown or Bohnanza.  Genoa is just too much too explain and too long running for our tastes it seems.  Traded away.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Undoubtedly one of the best negotiation games designed.  Unfortunately, it is just not the kind I enjoy.  Almost anything can be bartered and traded.  There are plenty of ways to achieve victory, and everything is equally useful if you can make the most of it (with the exception of the Palace/Building action special tile, which is dependent on you actually owning something on the board).  Full complement of players recommended, but be prepared for a long session.  I perfer open money as well, because it can give people a better idea as to who is currently in lead, but it is meaningless if no one take that into account when trading and is only interested in taking the highest offer.  Deeply involving and highly interactive, a must try for those into the social aspect of board gaming.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
An oldie amongst the new style euro-games. It took a long time before we played this one. Mainly because we just never were able to lay our hands on a copy. Until a couple of months ago, when we received an e-mail with an offer to buy this game (brand new, sealed) for 50% off. We ordered it, and it ended on one of our shelves. Didn’t expect too much of this game, but decided to give it a try and discover that way, why it’s ranked so high in the BGG-charts. After playing it became clear. This is a very good traders game. It’s more complex than most of the traders-games I know, and that’s exactly the reason why this isn’t just another solid pick-up and deliver game. It’s not a game for families, as it can take over two hours to play a game and it might too complex for those who don’t play games on regular base. But if you do play games more often, then this is a gem to have in your collection.

RATING: 7.9
Good game with potentially vicious trading.  Since you can trade anything physical in the game, it can get interesting, but usually the trading remains simple.  Also, the game does present a small suspense factor with the risk built into the market roll:  Unless the player chooses the start position rather than rolling, there is a 6.0125% chance that the turn track skips forward a turn..
